<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Petroleum</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Crude oil" redirects here. For the 2008 film, see <a href="/wiki/Crude_Oil_(film)" title="Crude Oil (film)">Crude Oil (film)</a>. For the fuel, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrol" title="Petrol">Petrol</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Petroleum_(disambiguation)" title="Petroleum (disambiguation)">Petroleum (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div refinery in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mina_Al_Ahmadi" title="Mina Al Ahmadi">Mina Al Ahmadi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Kor%C5%88ansk%C3%BD_ropn%C3%BD_prame%C5%88.JPG/330px-Kor%C5%88ansk%C3%BD_ropn%C3%BD_prame%C5%88.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Kor%C5%88ansk%C3%BD_ropn%C3%BD_prame%C5%88.JPG/440px-Kor%C5%88ansk%C3%BD_ropn%C3%BD_prame%C5%88.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Natural petroleum spring in <a href="/wiki/Kor%C5%88a" title="Korňa">Korňa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Petroleum</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Petroleum#Latent_heat_of_vaporization" title="Petroleum">naturally occurring</a>, yellow-to-black <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_formations" title="Geological formations">geological formations</a> beneath the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface. It is commonly refined into various types of <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a>. Components of petroleum are separated using a technique called <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>, i.e. separation of a liquid mixture into fractions differing in boiling point by means of distillation, typically using a <a href="/wiki/Fractionating_column" title="Fractionating column">fractionating column</a>.
</p><p>It consists of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> of various molecular weights and other <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The name <i>petroleum</i> covers both naturally occurring unprocessed <b>crude oil</b> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_product" title="Petroleum product">petroleum products</a> that are made up  of refined crude oil. A <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>, petroleum is formed when large quantities of dead organisms, usually <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, are buried underneath <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> and subjected to both intense heat and pressure.
</p><p>Petroleum has mostly been recovered by oil drilling (natural petroleum springs are rare). Drilling is carried out after studies of structural geology (at the reservoir scale), sedimentary basin analysis, and reservoir characterisation (mainly in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porosity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permeability_(earth_sciences)" title="Permeability (earth sciences)">permeability</a> of geologic reservoir structures) have been completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> It is refined and separated, most easily by <a href="/wiki/Continuous_distillation#Continuous_distillation_of_crude_oil" title="Continuous distillation">distillation</a>, into a large number of consumer products, from  <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (petrol) and <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> and chemical <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reagents</a> used to make <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" title="Pharmaceuticals">pharmaceuticals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixie_State_College_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixie_State_College-4">[4]</a></sup> Petroleum is used in manufacturing a wide variety of materials,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixie_State_College_2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixie_State_College_2-5">[5]</a></sup> and it is estimated that the world consumes about 95 million <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a> each day.
</p><p>Concern over the <a href="/wiki/Oil_depletion" title="Oil depletion">depletion</a> of the earth's <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resource" title="Non-renewable resource">finite reserves</a> of oil, and the effect this would have on a society dependent on it, is a concept known as <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>. The use of fossil fuels, such as petroleum, has a negative impact on Earth's biosphere, damaging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> through events such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> and releasing a range of pollutants into the air including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground-level_ozone" title="Ground-level ozone">ground-level ozone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> from sulfur impurities in fossil fuels. The burning of fossil fuels plays a major role in the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">current episode of global warming</a>.
</p>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2892" data-file-width="3856" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/220px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/330px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/440px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fractional distillation apparatus.</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>petroleum</i> comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">πέτρα</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">translit.</a> </small><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">petra</i>, "rock" and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/oleum#Latin" title="wikt:oleum">oleum</a></i>, "oil" from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ἔλαιον</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">translit.</a> </small><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">elaion</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term was was used in the treatise <i><a href="/wiki/De_Natura_Fossilium" title="De Natura Fossilium">De Natura Fossilium</a></i>, published in 1546 by the German mineralogist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Bauer" title="Georg Bauer">Georg Bauer</a>, also known as Georgius Agricola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the term <i>petroleum</i> was often used to refer to mineral oils produced by distillation from mined organic solids such as <a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">cannel coal</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>), and refined oils produced from them; in the United Kingdom, storage (and later transport) of these oils were regulated by a series of Petroleum Acts, from the <i>Petroleum Act 1863</i> onwards.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="History of the petroleum industry">History of the petroleum industry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="654" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/170px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/255px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/340px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil derrick in <a href="/wiki/Okemah,_Oklahoma" title="Okemah, Oklahoma">Okemah, Oklahoma</a>, 1922.</div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum, in one form or another, has been used since ancient times, and is now important across society, including in economy, politics and technology. The rise in importance was due to the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>, the rise in <a href="/wiki/Commercial_aviation" title="Commercial aviation">commercial aviation</a>, and the importance of petroleum to industrial organic chemistry, particularly the synthesis of plastics, fertilisers, solvents, adhesives and pesticides.
</p><p>More than 4000 years ago, according to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> was used in the construction of the walls and towers of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>; there were oil pits near Ardericca (near Babylon), and a pitch spring on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zacynthus" title="Zacynthus">Zacynthus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-12">[12]</a></sup> Great quantities of it were found on the banks of the river <a href="/wiki/Issus_(river)" title="Issus (river)">Issus</a>, one of the tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Persepolis_Administrative_Archives" title="Persepolis Administrative Archives">tablets</a> indicate the medicinal and lighting uses of petroleum in the upper levels of their society.
</p><p>The use of petroleum in ancient China dates back to more than 2000 years ago. In <a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a>, one of the earliest Chinese writings cites that oil in its raw state, without refining, was first discovered, extracted, and used in China in the first century BCE. In addition, the Chinese were the first to use petroleum as fuel as early as the fourth century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 347 AD, oil was produced from bamboo-drilled wells in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Early British explorers to <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> documented a flourishing oil extraction industry based in <a href="/wiki/Yenangyaung" title="Yenangyaung">Yenangyaung</a> that, in 1795, had hundreds of hand-dug wells under production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Merkwiller-Pechelbronn" title="Merkwiller-Pechelbronn">Pechelbronn</a> (Pitch fountain) is said to be the first European site where petroleum has been explored and used. The still active Erdpechquelle, a spring where petroleum appears mixed with water has been used since 1498, notably for medical purposes. Oil sands have been mined since the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Wietze" title="Wietze">Wietze</a> in lower Saxony, natural asphalt/bitumen has been explored since the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Both in Pechelbronn as in Wietze, the coal industry dominated the petroleum technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oil_Reserves.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="1799" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/400px-Oil_Reserves.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/600px-Oil_Reserves.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/800px-Oil_Reserves.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oil_Reserves.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proven world <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a>, 2013. Unconventional reservoirs such as natural heavy oil and <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a> are included.</div></div></div>
<p>Chemist <a href="/wiki/James_Young_(chemist)" title="James Young (chemist)">James Young</a> noticed a natural petroleum seepage in the <a href="/wiki/Riddings" title="Riddings">Riddings</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colliery" title="Colliery">colliery</a> at <a href="/wiki/Alfreton" title="Alfreton">Alfreton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a> from which he distilled a light thin oil suitable for use as lamp oil, at the same time obtaining a more viscous oil suitable for lubricating machinery. In 1848 Young set up a small business refining the crude oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Young eventually succeeded, by distilling <a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">cannel coal</a> at a low heat, in creating a fluid resembling petroleum, which when treated in the same way as the seep oil gave similar products. Young found that by slow distillation he could obtain a number of useful liquids from it, one of which he named "paraffine oil" because at low temperatures it congealed into a substance resembling paraffin wax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-russell-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production of these oils and solid <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin wax</a> from coal formed the subject of his patent dated 17 October 1850. In 1850 Young &amp; Meldrum and Edward William Binney entered into partnership under the title of E.W. Binney &amp; Co. at <a href="/wiki/Bathgate" title="Bathgate">Bathgate</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian" title="West Lothian">West Lothian</a> and E. Meldrum &amp; Co. at Glasgow; their works at Bathgate were completed in 1851 and became the first truly commercial oil-works in the world with the first modern oil refinery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> using oil extracted from locally mined <a href="/wiki/Torbanite" title="Torbanite">torbanite</a>, shale, and bituminous coal to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a> and lubricating oils; paraffin for fuel use and solid paraffin were not sold until 1856.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Lothian_shale_bing,_Scotland.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/220px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/330px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/440px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Lothian_shale_bing,_Scotland.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shale bings near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broxburn,_West_Lothian" title="Broxburn, West Lothian">Broxburn</a>, 3 of a total of 19 in <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian" title="West Lothian">West Lothian</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The world's first oil refinery was built in 1856 by <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_%C5%81ukasiewicz" title="Ignacy Łukasiewicz">Ignacy Łukasiewicz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> His achievements also included the discovery of how to distill kerosene from seep oil, the invention of the modern kerosene lamp (1853), the introduction of the first modern street lamp in Europe (1853), and the construction of the world's first modern oil well (1854).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The demand for petroleum as a fuel for lighting in North America and around the world quickly grew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Drake" title="Edwin Drake">Edwin Drake</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Drake_Well" title="Drake Well">1859 well</a> near Titusville, Pennsylvania, is popularly considered the first modern well. Already 1858 Georg Christian Konrad Hunäus had found a significant amount of petroleum while drilling for <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> 1858 in <a href="/wiki/Wietze" title="Wietze">Wietze</a>, Germany. Wietze later provided about 80% of the German consumption in the Wilhelminian Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The production stopped in 1963, but Wietze has hosted a Petroleum Museum since 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drake's well is probably singled out because it was drilled, not dug; because it used a steam engine; because there was a company associated with it; and because it touched off a major boom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> However, there was considerable activity before Drake in various parts of the world in the mid-19th century. A group directed by Major Alexeyev of the Bakinskii Corps of Mining Engineers hand-drilled a well in the Baku region in 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> There were engine-drilled wells in West Virginia in the same year as Drake's well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> An early commercial well was hand dug in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> in 1853, and another in nearby <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> in 1857. At around the same time the world's first, small, oil refinery was opened at <a href="/wiki/Jas%C5%82o" title="Jasło">Jasło</a> in Poland, with a larger one opened at <a href="/wiki/Ploie%C8%99ti" title="Ploiești">Ploiești</a> in Romania shortly after. Romania is the first country in the world to have had its annual crude oil output officially recorded in international statistics: 275 tonnes for 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry_in_Canada#Early_origins" title="History of the petroleum industry in Canada">first commercial oil well</a> in Canada became operational in 1858 at <a href="/wiki/Oil_Springs,_Ontario" title="Oil Springs, Ontario">Oil Springs, Ontario</a> (then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_West" title="Canada West">Canada West</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lclmg.org_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-lclmg.org-34">[34]</a></sup> Businessman <a href="/wiki/James_Miller_Williams" title="James Miller Williams">James Miller Williams</a> dug several wells between 1855 and 1858 before discovering a rich reserve of oil four metres below ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Unlear citation style.">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> Williams extracted 1.5 million litres of crude oil by 1860, refining much of it into kerosene lamp oil. Williams's well became commercially viable a year before Drake's Pennsylvania operation and could be argued to be the first commercial oil well in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The discovery at Oil Springs touched off an <a href="/wiki/Oil_boom" title="Oil boom">oil boom</a> which brought hundreds of speculators and workers to the area. Advances in drilling continued into 1862 when local driller Shaw reached a depth of 62 metres using the spring-pole drilling method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> On January 16, 1862, after an explosion of natural gas Canada's first oil gusher came into production, shooting into the air at a recorded rate of 3,000 barrels per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> By the end of the 19th century the Russian Empire, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a> company in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, had taken the lead in production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Akiner_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Akiner-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="360" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/170px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/255px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/340px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A poster used to promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpooling" title="Carpooling">carpooling</a> as a way to ration gasoline during World War II.</div></div></div>
<p>Access to oil was and still is a major factor in several military conflicts of the twentieth century, including <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, during which oil facilities were a major strategic asset and were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_Campaign_chronology_of_World_War_II" title="Oil Campaign chronology of World War II">extensively bombed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">German invasion of the Soviet Union</a> included the goal to capture the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Azerbaijan" title="Petroleum industry in Azerbaijan">Baku oilfields</a>, as it would provide much needed oil-supplies for the German military which was suffering from blockades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Oil exploration in North America during the early 20th century later led to the US becoming the leading producer by mid-century. As petroleum production in the US peaked during the 1960s, however, the United States was surpassed by Saudi Arabia and the Soviet Union.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Today, about 90 percent of vehicular fuel needs are met by oil. Petroleum also makes up 40 percent of total energy consumption in the United States, but is responsible for only 1 percent of electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Petroleum's worth as a portable, dense energy source powering the vast majority of vehicles and as the base of many industrial chemicals makes it one of the world's most important <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>. Viability of the oil commodity is controlled by several key parameters, number of vehicles in the world competing for fuel, quantity of oil exported to the world market (<a href="/wiki/Export_Land_Model" title="Export Land Model">Export Land Model</a>), net energy gain (economically useful energy provided minus energy consumed), political stability of oil exporting nations and ability to defend oil supply lines.ci<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The top three oil producing countries are Russia, Saudi Arabia and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2018, due in part to developments in <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Directional_drilling" title="Directional drilling">horizonal drilling</a>, the United States became the world's largest producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Energy_Information_Administration_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Energy_Information_Administration-45">[45]</a></sup> 
About 80 percent of the world's readily accessible reserves are located in the Middle East, with 62.5 percent coming from the Arab 5: <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>. A large portion of the world's total oil exists as unconventional sources, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">Athabasca oil sands</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_heavy_oil" title="Extra heavy oil">extra heavy oil</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco_Belt" title="Orinoco Belt">Orinoco Belt</a>. While significant volumes of oil are extracted from oil sands, particularly in Canada, logistical and technical hurdles remain, as oil extraction requires large amounts of heat and water, making its net energy content quite low relative to conventional crude oil. Thus, Canada's oil sands are not expected to provide more than a few million barrels per day in the foreseeable future.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In its strictest sense, petroleum includes only crude oil, but in common usage it includes all liquid, gaseous and solid <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>. Under surface <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">pressure and temperature conditions</a>, lighter hydrocarbons <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a> exist as gases, while <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> and heavier hydrocarbons are in the form of liquids or solids. However, in an underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" title="Oil reservoir">oil reservoir</a> the proportions of gas, liquid, and solid depend on subsurface conditions and on the <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> of the petroleum mixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil well</a> produces predominantly crude oil, with some natural gas <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">dissolved</a> in it. Because the pressure is lower at the surface than underground, some of the gas will come out of <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solution</a> and be recovered (or burned) as <i>associated gas</i> or <i>solution gas</i>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_well" title="Gas well">gas well</a> produces predominantly natural gas. However, because the underground temperature and pressure are higher than at the surface, the gas may contain heavier hydrocarbons such as pentane, <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaseous_state" title="Gaseous state">gaseous state</a>. At surface conditions these will <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condense</a> out of the gas to form "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_condensate" title="Natural gas condensate">natural gas condensate</a>", often shortened to <i>condensate.</i> Condensate resembles gasoline in appearance and is similar in composition to some <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> <a href="/wiki/Light_crude_oil" title="Light crude oil">light crude oils</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The proportion of light hydrocarbons in the petroleum mixture varies greatly among different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_fields" title="Oil fields">oil fields</a>, ranging from as much as 97 percent by weight in the lighter oils to as little as 50 percent in the heavier oils and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumens</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The hydrocarbons in crude oil are mostly <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, while the other organic compounds contain <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, and trace amounts of metals such as iron, nickel, copper and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>. Many oil reservoirs contain live bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The exact molecular composition of crude oil varies widely from formation to formation but the proportion of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> varies over fairly narrow limits as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>Four different types of hydrocarbon molecules appear in crude oil. The relative percentage of each varies from oil to oil, determining the properties of each oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="721" data-file-width="1281" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/220px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/330px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/440px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unconventional resources are much larger than conventional ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Crude oil varies greatly in appearance depending on its composition. It is usually black or dark brown (although it may be yellowish, reddish, or even greenish). In the reservoir it is usually found in association with natural gas, which being lighter forms a "gas cap" over the petroleum, and <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saline water</a> which, being heavier than most forms of crude oil, generally sinks beneath it. Crude oil may also be found in a semi-solid form mixed with sand and water, as in the <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">Athabasca oil sands</a> in Canada, where it is usually referred to as crude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>. In Canada, bitumen is considered a sticky, black, tar-like form of crude oil which is so thick and heavy that it must be heated or diluted before it will flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Venezuela also has large amounts of oil in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orinoco_oil_sands" title="Orinoco oil sands">Orinoco oil sands</a>, although the hydrocarbons trapped in them are more fluid than in Canada and are usually called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_heavy_oil" title="Extra heavy oil">extra heavy oil</a>. These oil sands resources are called <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> to distinguish them from oil which can be extracted using traditional oil well methods. Between them, Canada and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> contain an estimated 3.6 trillion barrels (570<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) of bitumen and extra-heavy oil, about twice the volume of the world's reserves of conventional oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Petroleum is used mostly, by volume, for refining into <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and gasoline, both important <i>"<a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a>"</i> sources. 84 percent by volume of the hydrocarbons present in petroleum is converted into energy-rich fuels (petroleum-based fuels), including gasoline, diesel, jet, heating, and other fuel oils, and <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The lighter grades of crude oil produce the best yields of these products, but as the world's reserves of light and medium oil are depleted, <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a> are increasingly having to process heavy oil and bitumen, and use more complex and expensive methods to produce the products required. Because heavier crude oils have too much carbon and not enough hydrogen, these processes generally involve removing carbon from or adding hydrogen to the molecules, and using <a href="/wiki/Fluid_catalytic_cracking" title="Fluid catalytic cracking">fluid catalytic cracking</a> to convert the longer, more complex molecules in the oil to the shorter, simpler ones in the fuels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Due to its high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a>, easy transportability and <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">relative abundance</a>, oil has become the world's most important source of energy since the mid-1950s. Petroleum is also the raw material for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> products, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceuticals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and plastics; the 16 percent not used for energy production is converted into these other materials. Petroleum is found in <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_formations" title="Rock formations">rock formations</a> in the upper <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a> of some areas of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>. There is also petroleum in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tar_sands" title="Tar sands">oil sands (tar sands)</a>. Known <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a> are typically estimated at around 190 km<sup>3</sup> (1.2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000000_(number)" title="1000000000000 (number)">trillion</a> <a href="/wiki/Long_and_short_scales" title="Long and short scales">(short scale)</a> <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a>) without oil sands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> or 595 km<sup>3</sup> (3.74 trillion barrels) with oil sands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Consumption is currently around 84 million barrels (13.4<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) per day, or 4.9 km<sup>3</sup> per year, yielding a remaining oil supply of only about 120 years, if current demand remains static.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octane_molecule_3D_model.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="1240" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/220px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/330px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/440px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octane_molecule_3D_model.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">Octane</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> found in petroleum. Lines represent <a href="/wiki/Single_bond" title="Single bond">single bonds</a>; black spheres represent <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>; white spheres represent <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum is a mixture of a very large number of different <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>; the most commonly found molecules are <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> (paraffins), <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naphthene" title="Naphthene">naphthenes</a>), <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, or more complicated chemicals like <a href="/wiki/Asphaltene" title="Asphaltene">asphaltenes</a>. Each petroleum variety has a unique mix of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, which define its physical and chemical properties, like color and <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>.
</p><p>The <i>alkanes</i>, also known as <i>paraffins</i>, are <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(chemistry)" title="Saturation (chemistry)">saturated</a> hydrocarbons with straight or branched chains which contain only <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and have the general formula C<sub>n</sub>H<sub>2n+2</sub>. They generally have from 5 to 40 carbon atoms per molecule, although trace amounts of shorter or longer molecules may be present in the mixture.
</p><p>The alkanes from <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub>) to <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a> (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub>) are <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">refined</a> into gasoline, the ones from <a href="/wiki/Nonane" title="Nonane">nonane</a> (C<sub>9</sub>H<sub>20</sub>) to <a href="/wiki/Hexadecane" title="Hexadecane">hexadecane</a> (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>34</sub>) into <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a>. Alkanes with more than 16 carbon atoms can be refined into <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lubricating_oil" title="Lubricating oil">lubricating oil</a>. At the heavier end of the range, <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin wax</a> is an alkane with approximately 25 carbon atoms, while <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> has 35 and up, although these are usually <a href="/wiki/Fluid_catalytic_cracking" title="Fluid catalytic cracking">cracked</a> by modern refineries into more valuable products. The shortest molecules, those with four or fewer carbon atoms, are in a gaseous state at room temperature. They are the petroleum gases. Depending on demand and the cost of recovery, these gases are either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flare_stack" title="Flare stack">flared off</a>, sold as <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> under pressure, or used to power the refinery's own burners. During the winter, butane (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub>), is blended into the gasoline pool at high rates, because its high vapor pressure assists with cold starts. Liquified under pressure slightly above atmospheric, it is best known for powering cigarette lighters, but it is also a main fuel source for many developing countries. Propane can be liquified under modest pressure, and is consumed for just about every application relying on petroleum for energy, from cooking to heating to transportation.
</p><p>The <i>cycloalkanes</i>, also known as <i>naphthenes</i>, are saturated hydrocarbons which have one or more carbon rings to which hydrogen atoms are attached according to the formula C<sub>n</sub>H<sub>2n</sub>. Cycloalkanes have similar properties to alkanes but have higher boiling points.
</p><p>The <i>aromatic hydrocarbons</i> are <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_unsaturation" title="Degree of unsaturation">unsaturated hydrocarbons</a> which have one or more planar six-carbon rings called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzene_ring" title="Benzene ring">benzene rings</a>, to which hydrogen atoms are attached with the formula C<sub>n</sub>H<sub>2n-6</sub>. They tend to burn with a sooty flame, and many have a sweet aroma. Some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a>.
</p><p>These different molecules are separated by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> at an oil refinery to produce gasoline, jet fuel, kerosene, and other hydrocarbons. For example, <a href="/wiki/2,2,4-Trimethylpentane" title="2,2,4-Trimethylpentane">2,2,4-trimethylpentane</a> (isooctane), widely used in gasoline, has a chemical formula of C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub> and it reacts with oxygen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermically</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>l</i>)</sub> + 25 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> → 16 <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> + 18 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> (ΔH = −5.51 MJ/mol of octane)</dd></dl>
<p>The number of various molecules in an oil sample can be determined by laboratory analysis. The molecules are typically extracted in a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, then separated in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_chromatograph" title="Gas chromatograph">gas chromatograph</a>, and finally determined with a suitable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detector" title="Detector">detector</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Flame_ionization_detector" title="Flame ionization detector">flame ionization detector</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">mass spectrometer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Due to the large number of co-eluted hydrocarbons within oil, many cannot be resolved by traditional gas chromatography and typically appear as a hump in the chromatogram. This <a href="/wiki/Unresolved_complex_mixture" title="Unresolved complex mixture">unresolved complex mixture</a> (UCM) of hydrocarbons is particularly apparent when analysing weathered oils and extracts from tissues of organisms exposed to oil. Some of the component of oil will mix with water: the <a href="/wiki/Water_associated_fraction" title="Water associated fraction">water associated fraction</a> of the oil.
</p><p>Incomplete combustion of petroleum or gasoline results in production of toxic byproducts. Too little oxygen during combustion results in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>. Due to the high temperatures and high pressures involved, exhaust gases from gasoline combustion in car engines usually include <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a> which are responsible for creation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_smog" title="Photochemical smog">photochemical smog</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Empirical_equations_for_thermal_properties">Empirical equations for thermal properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Empirical equations for thermal properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
