</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1769" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/300px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/450px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/600px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a vanadium <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a> compound (left) extracted from petroleum by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Treibs" title="Alfred E. Treibs">Alfred E. Treibs</a>, father of <a href="/wiki/Organic_geochemistry" title="Organic geochemistry">organic geochemistry</a>. Treibs noted the close structural similarity of this molecule and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_a" title="Chlorophyll a">chlorophyll a</a> (right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum is a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> derived from ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossilized" title="Fossilized">fossilized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic materials</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> Vast amounts of these remains settled to sea or lake bottoms where they were covered in <a href="/wiki/Water_stagnation" title="Water stagnation">stagnant water</a> (water with no dissolved <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mud" title="Mud">mud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> faster than they could <a href="/wiki/Decomposition#Anaerobic_vs_Aerobic" title="Decomposition">decompose aerobically</a>. Approximately 1 <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a> below this sediment or water oxygen concentration was low, below 0.1 mg/l, and <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">anoxic conditions</a> existed. Temperatures also remained constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>As further layers settled to the sea or lake bed, intense heat and pressure built up in the lower regions. This process caused the organic matter to change, first into a waxy material known as <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>, found in various <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shales</a> around the world, and then with more heat into liquid and gaseous <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> via a process known as <a href="/wiki/Catagenesis_(geology)" title="Catagenesis (geology)">catagenesis</a>. Formation of petroleum occurs from hydrocarbon <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> in a variety of mainly <a href="/wiki/Endothermic_process" title="Endothermic process">endothermic</a> reactions at high temperature or pressure, or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> These phases are described in detail below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaerobic_decay_or_1._phase_of_diagenesis">Anaerobic decay or 1. phase of diagenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Anaerobic decay or 1. phase of diagenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the absence of plentiful oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism"><i>aerobic</i> bacteria</a> were prevented from decaying the organic matter after it was buried under a layer of sediment or water. However, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism"><i>anaerobic</i> bacteria</a> were able to reduce <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> among the matter to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">H<sub>2</sub>S</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N<sub>2</sub></a> respectively by using the matter as a source for other reactants. Due to such anaerobic bacteria, at first this matter began to break apart mostly via <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>: <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> were hydrolyzed to <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">simple sugars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> respectively. These were further anaerobically <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidized</a> at an accelerated rate by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> of the bacteria: e.g. amino acids went through <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_deamination" title="Oxidative deamination">oxidative deamination</a> to <a href="/wiki/Imino_acid" title="Imino acid">imino acids</a>, which in turn reacted further to <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">α-keto acids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">Monosaccharides</a> in turn ultimately decayed to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. The anaerobic decay products of amino acids, monosaccharides, <a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">phenols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> combined to <a href="/wiki/Fulvic_acid" title="Fulvic acid">fulvic acids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">Fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxes</a> were not extensively hydrolyzed under these mild conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kerogen_formation_or_2._phase_of_diagenesis">Kerogen formation or 2. phase of diagenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Kerogen formation or 2. phase of diagenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some phenolic compounds produced from previous reactions worked as <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bactericides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actinomycetales" title="Actinomycetales">actinomycetales</a> order of bacteria produced antibiotic compounds (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Streptomycin" title="Streptomycin">streptomycin</a>). Thus the action of anaerobic bacteria ceased at about 10 m below the water or sediment. The mixture at this depth contained fulvic acids, unreacted and partially reacted fats and waxes, slightly modified <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, resins and other hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> As more layers of organic matter settled to the sea or lake bed, intense heat and pressure built up in the lower regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> As a consequence, compounds of this mixture the began to combine in poorly understood ways to <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>. Combination happened in a similar fashion as <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> molecules react to <a href="/wiki/Urea-formaldehyde" title="Urea-formaldehyde">urea-formaldehyde resins</a>, but kerogen formation occurred in a more complex manner due to a bigger variety of reactants. The total process of kerogen formation from the beginning of anaerobic decay is called <b>diagenesis</b>, a word that means a transformation of materials by dissolution and recombination of their constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kerogen_to_fossil_fuels_or_catagenesis">Kerogen to fossil fuels or catagenesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kerogen to fossil fuels or catagenesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kerogen formation continued to the depth of about 1 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a> from the Earth's surface where temperatures may reach around 50 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>. Kerogen formation represents a halfway point between organic matter and fossil fuels: kerogen can be exposed to oxygen, oxidize and thus be lost or it could be buried deeper inside the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a> and be subjected to conditions which allow it to slowly transform into fossil fuels like petroleum. The latter happened through <b>catagenesis</b> in which the reactions were mostly <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a> <a href="/wiki/Rearrangement_reaction" title="Rearrangement reaction">rearrangements</a> of kerogen. These reactions took thousands to millions of years and no external reactants were involved. Due to radical nature of these reactions, kerogen reacted towards two classes of products: those with low H/C ratio (<a href="/wiki/Anthracene" title="Anthracene">anthracene</a> or products similar to it) and those with high H/C ratio (<a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> or products similar to it); i.e. carbon-rich or hydrogen-rich products. Because catagenesis was closed off from external reactants, the resulting composition of the fuel mixture was dependent on the composition of the kerogen via reaction <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>. 3 main types of kerogen exist: type I (algal), II (liptinic) and III (humic), which were formed mainly from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woody_plant" title="Woody plant">woody plants</a> (this term includes <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">shrubs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liana" title="Liana">lianas</a>) respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Catagenesis was <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolytic</a> despite of the fact that it happened at relatively low temperatures (when compared to commercial pyrolysis plants) of 60 to several hundred °C. Pyrolysis was possible because of the long reaction times involved. Heat for catagenesis came from the decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> materials of the crust, especially <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232"><sup>232</sup>Th</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>. The heat varied with <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">geothermal gradient</a> and was typically 10-30 °C per km of depth from the Earth's surface. Unusual <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> intrusions, however, could have created greater localized heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-7"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Geologists often refer to the temperature range in which oil forms as an "oil window".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-8"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> Below the minimum temperature oil remains trapped in the form of kerogen. Above the maximum temperature the oil is converted to natural gas through the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_cracking" title="Thermal cracking">thermal cracking</a>. Sometimes, oil formed at extreme depths may migrate and become trapped at a much shallower level. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca Oil Sands</a> are one example of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-9"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abiogenic_petroleum">Abiogenic petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Abiogenic petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alternative mechanism to the one described above was proposed by Russian scientists in the mid-1850s, the hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Abiogenic_petroleum_origin" title="Abiogenic petroleum origin">abiogenic petroleum origin</a> (petroleum formed by inorganic means), but this is contradicted by geological and <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemical</a> evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glasby2006_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-glasby2006-66">[66]</a></sup> Abiogenic sources of oil have been found, but never in commercially profitable amounts. "The controversy isn't over whether abiogenic oil reserves exist," said Larry Nation of the American Association of Petroleum Geologists. "The controversy is over how much they contribute to Earth's overall reserves and how much time and effort geologists should devote to seeking them out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reservoirs">Reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(October 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Structural_Trap_(Anticlinal).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="640" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/220px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/330px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/440px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Structural_Trap_(Anticlinal).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Hydrocarbon trap.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Three conditions must be present for oil reservoirs to form:
</p>
<ul><li>a <a href="/wiki/Source_rock" title="Source rock">source rock</a> rich in <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> material buried deeply enough for subterranean heat to cook it into oil,</li>
<li>a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous">porous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permeability_(fluid)" title="Permeability (fluid)">permeable</a> reservoir rock where it can  accumulate,</li>
<li>a <a href="/wiki/Caprock" title="Caprock">caprock</a> (seal) or other mechanism to prevent the oil from escaping to the surface. Within these reservoirs, fluids will typically organize themselves like a three-layer cake with a layer of water below the oil layer and a layer of gas above it, although the different layers vary in size between reservoirs. Because most hydrocarbons are less dense than rock or water, they often migrate upward through adjacent rock layers until either reaching the surface or becoming trapped within porous rocks (known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" title="Oil reservoir">reservoirs</a>) by impermeable rocks above. However, the process is influenced by underground water flows, causing oil to migrate hundreds of kilometres horizontally or even short distances downward before becoming trapped in a reservoir. When hydrocarbons are concentrated in a trap, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil field</a> forms, from which the liquid can be extracted by <a href="/wiki/Drill" title="Drill">drilling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumping</a>.</li></ul>
<p>The reactions that produce oil and natural gas are often modeled as first order breakdown reactions, where hydrocarbons are broken down to oil and natural gas by a set of parallel reactions, and oil eventually breaks down to natural gas by another set of reactions. The latter set is regularly used in <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a> plants and <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a>.
</p><p>Wells are drilled into oil reservoirs to extract the crude oil. "Natural lift" production methods that rely on the natural reservoir pressure to force the oil to the surface are usually sufficient for a while after reservoirs are first tapped. In some reservoirs, such as in the Middle East, the natural pressure is sufficient over a long time. The natural pressure in most reservoirs, however, eventually dissipates. Then the oil must be extracted using "<a href="/wiki/Artificial_lift" title="Artificial lift">artificial lift</a>" means. Over time, these "primary" methods become less effective and "secondary" production methods may be used. A common secondary method is <a href="/wiki/Water_injection_(oil_production)" title="Water injection (oil production)">"waterflood"</a> or injection of water into the reservoir to increase pressure and force the oil to the drilled shaft or "wellbore." Eventually "tertiary" or "enhanced" oil recovery methods may be used to increase the oil's flow characteristics by injecting steam, carbon dioxide and other gases or chemicals into the reservoir. In the United States, primary production methods account for less than 40 percent of the oil produced on a daily basis, secondary methods account for about half, and tertiary recovery the remaining 10 percent. Extracting oil (or "bitumen") from oil/tar sand and oil shale deposits requires mining the sand or shale and heating it in a vessel or retort, or using "in-situ" methods of injecting heated liquids into the deposit and then pumping the liquid back out saturated with oil.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_oil_reservoirs">Unconventional oil reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Unconventional oil reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">Unconventional oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale_reserves" title="Oil shale reserves">Oil shale reserves</a></div>
<p>Oil-eating bacteria <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegrade</a> oil that has escaped to the surface. <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a> are reservoirs of partially biodegraded oil still in the process of escaping and being biodegraded, but they contain so much migrating oil that, although most of it has escaped, vast amounts are still present—more than can be found in conventional oil reservoirs. The lighter fractions of the crude oil are destroyed first, resulting in reservoirs containing an extremely heavy form of crude oil, called crude bitumen in Canada, or extra-heavy crude oil in <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>. These two countries have the world's largest deposits of oil sands.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_shales" title="Oil shales">oil shales</a> are source rocks that have not been exposed to heat or pressure long enough to convert their trapped hydrocarbons into crude oil. Technically speaking, oil shales are not always shales and do not contain oil, but are fined-grain sedimentary rocks containing an insoluble organic solid called <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>. The kerogen in the rock can be converted into crude oil using heat and pressure to simulate natural processes. The method has been known for centuries and was patented in 1694 under British Crown Patent No. 330 covering, "A way to extract and make great quantities of pitch, tar, and oil out of a sort of stone." Although oil shales are found in many countries, the United States has the world's largest deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambertson_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambertson-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crudes.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="459" height="384" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crudes.PNG/350px-Crudes.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Crudes.PNG 1.5x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crudes.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">marker crudes</a> with their <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> content (horizontal) and <a href="/wiki/API_gravity" title="API gravity">API gravity</a> (vertical) and relative production quantity.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">Benchmark (crude oil)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">petroleum industry</a> generally classifies crude oil by the geographic location it is produced in (e.g. <a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">West Texas Intermediate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brent_oilfield" title="Brent oilfield">Brent</a>, or <a href="/wiki/DME_Oman_Crude_Oil_Futures_Contract" title="DME Oman Crude Oil Futures Contract">Oman</a>), its <a href="/wiki/API_gravity" title="API gravity">API gravity</a> (an oil industry measure of density), and its sulfur content. Crude oil may be considered <i><a href="/wiki/Light_crude_oil" title="Light crude oil">light</a></i> if it has low density or <i><a href="/wiki/Heavy_crude_oil" title="Heavy crude oil">heavy</a></i> if it has high density; and it may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Sweet_crude_oil" title="Sweet crude oil">sweet</a> if it contains relatively little sulfur or <i><a href="/wiki/Sour_crude_oil" title="Sour crude oil">sour</a></i> if it contains substantial amounts of sulfur.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The geographic location is important because it affects transportation costs to the refinery. <i>Light</i> crude oil is more desirable than <i>heavy</i> oil since it produces a higher yield of gasoline, while <i>sweet</i> oil commands a higher price than <i>sour</i> oil because it has fewer environmental problems and requires less refining to meet sulfur standards imposed on fuels in consuming countries. Each crude oil has unique molecular characteristics which are revealed by the use of <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil_assay" title="Crude oil assay">Crude oil assay</a> analysis in petroleum laboratories.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">Barrels</a> from an area in which the crude oil's molecular characteristics have been determined and the oil has been classified are used as pricing <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">references</a> throughout the world. Some of the common reference crudes are:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">West Texas Intermediate</a> (WTI), a very high-quality, sweet, light oil delivered at <a href="/wiki/Cushing,_Oklahoma" title="Cushing, Oklahoma">Cushing, Oklahoma</a> for North American oil</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">Brent Blend</a>, consisting of 15 oils from fields in the <a href="/wiki/Brent_oilfield" title="Brent oilfield">Brent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ninian" title="Ninian">Ninian</a> systems in the <a href="/wiki/East_Shetland_Basin" title="East Shetland Basin">East Shetland Basin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. The oil is landed at <a href="/wiki/Sullom_Voe" title="Sullom Voe">Sullom Voe</a> terminal in <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>. Oil production from Europe, Africa and Middle Eastern oil flowing West tends to be priced off this oil, which forms a <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">benchmark</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dubai_Crude" title="Dubai Crude">Dubai-Oman</a>, used as benchmark for Middle East sour crude oil flowing to the Asia-Pacific region</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tapis_crude" title="Tapis crude">Tapis</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, used as a reference for light Far East oil)</li>
<li>Minas (from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, used as a reference for heavy Far East oil)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/OPEC_Reference_Basket" title="OPEC Reference Basket">OPEC Reference Basket</a>, a weighted average of oil blends from various <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> (The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries) countries</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Midway-Sunset_Oil_Field" title="Midway-Sunset Oil Field">Midway Sunset</a> Heavy, by which heavy oil in California is priced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2018)">not in citations given</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_Canadian_Select" title="Western Canadian Select">Western Canadian Select</a>  the benchmark crude oil for emerging heavy, high TAN (acidic) crudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRCPetroleumProducts_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRCPetroleumProducts-70">[70]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There are declining amounts of these benchmark oils being produced each year, so other oils are more commonly what is actually delivered. While the reference price may be for West Texas Intermediate delivered at Cushing, the actual oil being traded may be a discounted Canadian heavy oil—Western Canadian Select— delivered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardisty,_Alberta" title="Hardisty, Alberta">Hardisty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, and for a Brent Blend delivered at Shetland, it may be a discounted Russian Export Blend delivered at the port of <a href="/wiki/Primorsk,_Leningrad_Oblast" title="Primorsk, Leningrad Oblast">Primorsk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_industry">Petroleum industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Petroleum industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(April 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">Petroleum industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="586" data-file-width="753" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/300px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/450px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/600px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crude oil export treemap (2012) from Harvard Atlas of Economic Complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="548" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/220px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/330px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/440px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a> prices ($/bbl) for West Texas Intermediate 2000 through Oct 2014.</div></div></div>
<p>The petroleum industry is involved in the global processes of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_exploration" title="Hydrocarbon exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">extraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">refining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_transport" title="Petroleum transport">transporting</a> (often with <a href="/wiki/Oil_tanker" title="Oil tanker">oil tankers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>), and marketing petroleum products. The largest volume products of the industry are <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and gasoline. Petroleum is also the raw material for many <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">chemical products</a>, including pharmaceuticals, solvents, fertilizers, pesticides, and plastics. The industry is usually divided into three major components: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upstream_(oil_industry)" title="Upstream (oil industry)">upstream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midstream" title="Midstream">midstream</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downstream_(oil_industry)" title="Downstream (oil industry)">downstream</a>. Midstream operations are usually included in the downstream category.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Petroleum is vital to many industries, and is of importance to the maintenance of industrialized <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> itself, and thus is a critical concern to many nations. Oil accounts for a large percentage of the world's energy consumption, ranging from a low of 32 percent for Europe and Asia, up to a high of 53 percent for the Middle East, South and Central America (44%), Africa (41%), and North America (40%). The world at large consumes 30 billion <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a> (4.8 km³) of oil per year, and the top oil consumers largely consist of developed nations. In fact, 24 percent of the oil consumed in 2004 went to the United States alone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> though by 2007 this had dropped to 21 percent of world oil consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the US, in the states of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, California, Hawaii, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Western_States_Petroleum_Association" title="Western States Petroleum Association">Western States Petroleum Association</a> (WSPA) represents companies responsible for producing, distributing, refining, transporting and marketing petroleum. This non-profit trade association was founded in 1907, and is the oldest petroleum trade association in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Shipping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1950s, shipping costs made up 33 percent of the price of oil transported from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> to the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist.com_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-76">[76]</a></sup> but due to the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supertankers" title="Supertankers">supertankers</a> in the 1970s, the cost of shipping dropped to only 5 percent of the price of Persian oil in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist.com_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-76">[76]</a></sup> Due to the increase of the value of the crude oil during the last 30 years, the share of the shipping cost on the final cost of the delivered commodity was less than 3% in 2010. For example, in 2010 the shipping cost from the Persian Gulf to the US was in the range of 20 $/t and the cost of the delivered crude oil around 800 $/t.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Price">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1155" data-file-width="1764" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/220px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/330px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/440px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nominal and inflation-adjusted US dollar price of crude oil, 1861–2015.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Price_of_oil" title="Price of oil">Price of oil</a></div>
<p>After the collapse of the OPEC-administered pricing system in 1985, and a short-lived experiment with netback pricing, oil-exporting countries adopted a market-linked pricing mechanism. First adopted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PEMEX" title="PEMEX">PEMEX</a> in 1986, market-linked pricing was widely accepted, and by 1988 became and still is the main method for pricing crude oil in international trade. The current reference, or pricing markers, are <a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">Brent</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">WTI</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Crude" title="Dubai Crude">Dubai/Oman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_product" title="Petroleum product">Petroleum product</a></div>
<p>The chemical structure of petroleum is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterogeneity" title="Heterogeneity">heterogeneous</a>, composed of hydrocarbon chains of different lengths. Because of this, petroleum may be taken to <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a> and the hydrocarbon chemicals separated by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> and treated by other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_process" title="Chemical process">chemical processes</a>, to be used for a variety of purposes.  The total cost per plant is about 9 billion dollars.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuels">Fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">distillation fractions</a> of petroleum are <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a>. Fuels include (by increasing boiling temperature range):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_derivatives">Other derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Certain types of resultant hydrocarbons may be mixed with other non-hydrocarbons, to create other end products:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkenes" title="Alkenes">Alkenes</a> (olefins), which can be manufactured into plastics or other compounds</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">Lubricants</a> (produces light machine oils, <a href="/wiki/Motor_oil" title="Motor oil">motor oils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grease_(lubricant)" title="Grease (lubricant)">greases</a>, adding <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> stabilizers as required)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">Wax</a>, used in the packaging of <a href="/wiki/Frozen_food" title="Frozen food">frozen foods</a>, among others</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">Sulfur</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. These are useful industrial materials. Sulfuric acid is usually prepared as the acid precursor <a href="/wiki/Oleum" title="Oleum">oleum</a>, a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">sulfur removal</a> from fuels.</li>
<li>Bulk <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">Asphalt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a>, used in speciality carbon products or as solid fuel</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">Paraffin wax</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">Aromatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a> to be used as precursors in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> production</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1940s, agricultural productivity has increased dramatically, due largely to the increased use of energy-intensive <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">mechanization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_by_country">Petroleum by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Petroleum by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption_statistics">Consumption statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Consumption statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 195px"><div style="width: 195px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 190px;"><div style="margin:25px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/160px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/240px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/320px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
Global fossil carbon emissions, an indicator of consumption, from 1800. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Total</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:blue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Oil</div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the US Energy Information Administration (EIA) estimate for 2011, the world consumes 87.421 million barrels of oil each day.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/750px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/1125px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png 2x" width="750"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil consumption per capita (darker colors represent more consumption, gray represents no data) <i>(source: see file description)</i>. <table style="width: 100%; font-size: 1em;"> <tbody><tr> <td valign="top"> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#800000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span>  &gt; 0.07</span><br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#a52a2a; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> 0.07 - 0.05</span><br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ff0000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> 0.05 - 0.035</span><br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#fa8072; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> 0.035 - 0.025</span><br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#f08080; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> 0.025 - 0.02</span><br/> </td> <td valign="top"> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffebda; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> 0.015 - 0.01</span><br/> <span style="margin:0; 0.0015</span><br/> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For oil production by country, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_production" title="List of countries by oil production">List of countries by oil production</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For oil reserves by country, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_proven_oil_reserves" title="List of countries by proven oil reserves">List of countries by proven oil reserves</a>.</div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="681" height="435" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png/400px-Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png/600px-Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Top_Oil_Producing_Countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Top oil-producing countries (million barrels per day).</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oil_producing_countries_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1427" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png/750px-Oil_producing_countries_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png/1125px-Oil_producing_countries_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png 2x" width="750"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oil_producing_countries_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">World map with <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_production" title="List of countries by oil production">countries by oil production</a> (information from 2006–2012).</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In petroleum industry parlance, <i>production</i> refers to the quantity of crude extracted from reserves, not the literal creation of the product.

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Export">Export</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Export">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_exporters" title="Fossil fuel exporters">Fossil fuel exporters</a> and <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:612px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_Petroleum_Countries_Export_Treemap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="750" height="423" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2014_Petroleum_Countries_Export_Treemap.png/610px-2014_Petroleum_Countries_Export_Treemap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/2014_Petroleum_Countries_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x" width="610"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_Petroleum_Countries_Export_Treemap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Petroleum Exports by Country (2014) from <a class="external text" href="http://atlas.cid.harvard.edu/explore/tree_map/export/show/all/2709/2014/" rel="nofollow">Harvard Atlas of Economic Complexity</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="Environmental impact of the petroleum industry">Environmental impact of the petroleum industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="871" data-file-width="976" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/220px-Dieselrainbow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/330px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/440px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diesel fuel spill on a road.</div></div></div>
<p>Because petroleum is a naturally occurring substance, its presence in the environment need not be the result of human causes such as accidents and routine activities (<a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismic</a> exploration, <a href="/wiki/Boring_(earth)" title="Boring (earth)">drilling</a>, extraction, refining and combustion). Phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_seep" title="Petroleum seep">seeps</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tar_pit" title="Tar pit">tar pits</a> are examples of areas that petroleum affects without man's involvement. Regardless of source, petroleum's effects when released into the environment are similar.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_acidification">Ocean acidification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ocean acidification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="540" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/220px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/330px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/440px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seawater acidification.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a> is the increase in the acidity of the Earth's oceans caused by the uptake of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. This increase in acidity inhibits all marine life – having a greater impact on smaller organisms as well as shelled organisms (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scallops" title="Scallops">scallops</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming">Global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When burned, petroleum releases carbon dioxide, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>. Along with the burning of coal, petroleum combustion may be the largest contributor to the increase in atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> has risen over the last 150 years to current levels of over 390 <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppmv</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere#Past_variation" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">180 – 300 ppmv of the prior 800 thousand years</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This rise in temperature may have reduced the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">Arctic ice cap</a> to 1,100,000 sq mi (2,800,000 km<sup>2</sup>),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> smaller than ever recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Because of this melt, more oil reserves have been revealed. About 13 percent of the world's undiscovered oil resides in the Arctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GautierBird2009_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-GautierBird2009-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraction">Extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oil extraction is simply the removal of oil from the reservoir (oil pool). Oil is often recovered as a water-in-oil emulsion, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialty_chemicals" title="Specialty chemicals">specialty chemicals</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demulsifiers" title="Demulsifiers">demulsifiers</a> are used to separate the oil from water. Oil extraction is costly and sometimes environmentally damaging. Offshore exploration and extraction of oil disturbs the surrounding marine environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_spills">Oil spills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Oil spills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">Oil spill</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil_spills" title="List of oil spills">List of oil spills</a></div>
