//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg/460px-Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Oil slick from the <a href="/wiki/Montara_oil_spill" title="Montara oil spill">Montara oil spill</a> in the Timor Sea, September, 2009.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="199" data-file-width="289" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg/230px-PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg 1.5x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Volunteers cleaning up the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_oil_spill" title="Prestige oil spill">Prestige oil spill</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Crude oil and refined fuel <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">spills</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">tanker ship</a> accidents have damaged natural <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">Galápagos Islands</a>, France and many <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil_spills" title="List of oil spills">other places</a>.
</p><p>The quantity of oil spilled during accidents has ranged from a few hundred tons to several hundred thousand tons (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a>, <a href="/wiki/SS_Atlantic_Empress" title="SS Atlantic Empress">SS Atlantic Empress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amoco_Cadiz" title="Amoco Cadiz">Amoco Cadiz</a>). Smaller spills have already proven to have a great impact on ecosystems, such as the <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Valdez_oil_spill" title="Exxon Valdez oil spill"><i>Exxon Valdez</i> oil spill</a>.
</p><p>Oil spills at sea are generally much more damaging than those on land, since they can spread for hundreds of nautical miles in a thin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_slick" title="Oil slick">oil slick</a> which can cover beaches with a thin coating of oil. This can kill sea birds, mammals, shellfish and other organisms it coats. Oil spills on land are more readily containable if a makeshift earth dam can be rapidly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulldozed" title="Bulldozed">bulldozed</a> around the spill site before most of the oil escapes, and land animals can avoid the oil more easily.
</p><p>Control of oil spills is difficult, requires ad hoc methods, and often a large amount of manpower. The dropping of bombs and incendiary devices from aircraft on the <a href="/wiki/SS_Torrey_Canyon" title="SS Torrey Canyon">SS <i>Torrey Canyon</i></a> wreck produced poor results;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> modern techniques would include pumping the oil from the wreck, like in the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_oil_spill" title="Prestige oil spill"><i>Prestige</i> oil spill</a> or the <a href="/wiki/MV_Erika" title="MV Erika"><i>Erika</i></a> oil spill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though crude oil is predominantly composed of various hydrocarbons, certain nitrogen heterocylic compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Picoline" title="Picoline">picoline</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quinoline" title="Quinoline">quinoline</a> are reported as contaminants associated with crude oil, as well as facilities processing oil shale or coal, and have also been found at legacy <a href="/wiki/Creosote" title="Creosote">wood treatment</a> sites. These compounds have a very high water solubility, and thus tend to dissolve and move with water. Certain naturally occurring bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Micrococcus" title="Micrococcus">Micrococcus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Arthrobacter" title="Arthrobacter">Arthrobacter</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodococcus" title="Rhodococcus">Rhodococcus</a></i> have been shown to degrade these contaminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tarballs">Tarballs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Tarballs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A tarball is a blob of crude oil (not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>, which is a man-made product derived from pine trees or refined from petroleum) which has been weathered after floating in the ocean. Tarballs are an aquatic <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutant</a> in most environments, although they can occur naturally, for example in the Santa Barbara Channel of California<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itah_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-itah-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hostettler_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-hostettler-98">[98]</a></sup> or in the Gulf of Mexico off Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Their concentration and features have been used to assess the extent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_spills" title="Oil spills">oil spills</a>. Their composition can be used to identify their sources of origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and tarballs themselves may be dispersed over long distances by deep sea currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hostettler_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-hostettler-98">[98]</a></sup> They are slowly decomposed by bacteria, including <i><a href="/wiki/Chromobacterium_violaceum" title="Chromobacterium violaceum">Chromobacterium violaceum</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cladosporium_resinae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cladosporium resinae (page does not exist)">Cladosporium resinae</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bacillus_submarinus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bacillus submarinus (page does not exist)">Bacillus submarinus</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Micrococcus_varians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Micrococcus varians (page does not exist)">Micrococcus varians</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Candida_marina&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Candida marina (page does not exist)">Candida marina</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saccharomyces_estuari&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saccharomyces estuari (page does not exist)">Saccharomyces estuari</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itah_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-itah-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whales">Whales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Whales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>James S. Robbins has argued that the advent of petroleum-refined kerosene saved some species of great whales from <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> by providing an inexpensive substitute for <a href="/wiki/Whale_oil" title="Whale oil">whale oil</a>, thus eliminating the economic imperative for open-boat <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_petroleum">Alternatives to petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Alternatives to petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a></div>
<p>In the United States in 2007 about 70 percent of petroleum was used for transportation (e.g. gasoline, diesel, jet fuel), 24 percent by industry (e.g. production of plastics), 5 percent for residential and commercial uses, and 2 percent for electricity production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Outside of the US, a higher proportion of petroleum tends to be used for electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_petroleum-based_vehicle_fuels">Alternatives to petroleum-based vehicle fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Alternatives to petroleum-based vehicle fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel_vehicle" title="Alternative fuel vehicle">Alternative fuel vehicle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen economy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_vehicle" title="Green vehicle">Green vehicle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/220px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/330px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/440px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> fuel station with four <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a> for sale: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> (B3), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasohol" title="Gasohol">gasohol</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasohol#E20,_E25" title="Gasohol">E25</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">neat ethanol</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasohol#E100" title="Gasohol">E100</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG).</div></div></div>
<p>Alternative fuel vehicles refers to both:
</p>
<ul><li>Vehicles that use <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a> used in standard or modified <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">natural gas vehicles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_vehicle" title="Neat ethanol vehicle">neat ethanol vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>-powered vehicles, propane autogas, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicle">hydrogen vehicles</a>.</li>
<li>Vehicles with advanced propulsion systems that reduce or substitute petroleum use such as <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">plug-in hybrid electric vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid electric vehicles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicles</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_using_oil_in_industry">Alternatives to using oil in industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Alternatives to using oil in industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biological feedstocks do exist for industrial uses such as <a href="/wiki/Bioplastic" title="Bioplastic">Bioplastic</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_burning_petroleum_for_electricity">Alternatives to burning petroleum for electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Alternatives to burning petroleum for electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_energy" title="Alternative energy">Alternative energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a></div>
<p>In oil producing countries with little refinery capacity, oil is sometimes burned to produce electricity. <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micro_hydro" title="Micro hydro">micro hydro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> are used, but the primary alternatives remain large-scale <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a> and coal-fired generation.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_petroleum_production">Future of petroleum production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Future of petroleum production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="750" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/220px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/330px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/440px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US oil production and imports, 1910-2012.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Consumption_function" title="Consumption function">Consumption</a> in the twentieth and twenty-first centuries has been abundantly pushed by automobile sector growth. The <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">1985–2003 oil glut</a> even fueled the sales of low fuel economy vehicles in <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries. The 2008 economic crisis seems to have had some impact on the sales of such vehicles; still, in 2008 oil consumption showed a small increase.
</p><p>In 2016 Goldman Sachs predicted lower demand for oil due to emerging economies concerns, especially China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BRICS" title="BRICS">BRICS</a> (Brasil, Russia, India, China, South Africa) countries might also kick in, as China briefly was the first automobile market in December 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The immediate outlook still hints upwards. In the long term, uncertainties linger; the <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> believes that the OECD countries will push low consumption policies at some point in the future; when that happens, it will definitely curb oil sales, and both OPEC and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> (EIA) kept lowering their 2020 consumption estimates during the past five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> A detailed review of <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> oil projections have revealed that revisions of world oil production, price and investments have been motivated by a combination of demand and supply factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wachtmeister2018_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wachtmeister2018-109">[109]</a></sup> All together, Non-OPEC conventional projections have been fairly stable the last 15 years, while downward revisions were mainly allocated to OPEC. Recent upward revisions are primarily a result of US <a href="/wiki/Tight_oil" title="Tight oil">tight oil</a>.
</p><p>Production will also face an increasingly complex situation; while OPEC countries still have large reserves at low production prices, newly found reservoirs often lead to higher prices; offshore giants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tupi_oil_field" title="Tupi oil field">Tupi</a>, Guara and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiber_oilfield" title="Tiber oilfield">Tiber</a> demand high investments and ever-increasing technological abilities. Subsalt reservoirs such as Tupi were unknown in the twentieth century, mainly because the industry was unable to probe them. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Oil_Recovery" title="Enhanced Oil Recovery">Enhanced Oil Recovery</a> (EOR) techniques (example: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daqing_Field" title="Daqing Field">DaQing</a>, China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> ) will continue to play a major role in increasing the world's recoverable oil.
</p><p>The expected availability of petroleum resources has always been around 35 years or even less since the start of the modern exploration. The <a href="/wiki/Oil_constant" title="Oil constant">oil constant</a>, an insider pun in the German industry, refers to that effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peak_oil">Peak oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Peak oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">Peak oil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="600" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg/300px-GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg/450px-GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global peak oil forecast.</div></div></div>
<p>Peak oil is a term applied to the projection that future petroleum production (whether for individual oil wells, entire oil fields, whole countries, or worldwide production) will eventually peak and then decline at a similar rate to the rate of increase before the peak as these reserves are exhausted. The peak of oil discoveries was in 1965, and oil production per year has surpassed oil discoveries every year since 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> However, this does not mean that potential oil production has surpassed oil demand.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marion_King_Hubbert" title="Marion King Hubbert">Hubbert</a> applied his theory to accurately predict the peak of U.S. conventional oil production at a date between 1966 and 1970. This prediction was based on data available at the time of his publication in 1956. In the same paper, Hubbert predicts world peak oil in "half a century" after his publication, which would be 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is difficult to predict the oil peak in any given region, due to the lack of knowledge and/or transparency in accounting of global oil reserves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>  Based on available production data, proponents have previously predicted the peak for the world to be in years 1989, 1995, or 1995–2000. Some of these predictions date from before the recession of the early 1980s, and the consequent reduction in global consumption, the effect of which was to delay the date of any peak by several years. Just as the 1971 U.S. peak in oil production was only clearly recognized after the fact, a peak in world production will be difficult to discern until production clearly drops off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The peak is also a moving target as it is now measured as "liquids", which includes synthetic fuels, instead of just conventional oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) said in 2010 that production of conventional crude oil had peaked in 2006 at 70 MBBL/d, then flattened at 68 or 69 thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Since virtually all economic sectors rely heavily on petroleum, <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, if it were to occur, could lead to a "partial or complete failure of markets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In the mid-2000s, widespread fears of an imminent peak led to the "peak oil movement," in which over one hundred thousand Americans prepared, individually and collectively, for the "post-carbon" future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_production">Unconventional production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Unconventional production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unconventional oil is petroleum produced or extracted using techniques other than the conventional methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The calculus for peak oil has changed with the introduction of unconventional production methods. In particular, the combination of <a href="/wiki/Directional_drilling" title="Directional drilling">horizontal drilling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> has resulted in a significant increase in production from previously uneconomic plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Analysts expected that $150 billion would be spent on further developing North American tight oil fields in 2015. The large increase in tight oil production is one of the reasons behind the price drop in late 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Certain rock <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strata" title="Strata">strata</a> contain hydrocarbons but have low permeability and are not thick from a vertical perspective. Conventional vertical wells would be unable to economically retrieve these hydrocarbons. Horizontal drilling, extending horizontally through the strata, permits the well to access a much greater volume of the strata. Hydraulic fracturing creates greater permeability and increases hydrocarbon flow to the wellbore.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">oil - late 12c., "olive oil," from Anglo-French and Old North French olie, from Old French oile, uile "oil" (12c., Modern French huile), from Latin oleum "oil, olive oil" (source of Spanish, Italian olio), from Greek elaion "olive tree," from elaia <i><a class="external free" href="https://www.etymonline.com/word/oil" rel="nofollow">https://www.etymonline.com/word/oil</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180309120415/https://www.etymonline.com/word/oil" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> March 9, 2018, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Medieval Latin: literally, rock oil = Latin petr(a) rock (&lt; Greek pétra) + oleum oil <i><a class="external free" href="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/petroleum" rel="nofollow">http://www.thefreedictionary.com/petroleum</a></i></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Cheraghian, Goshtasp (2017). <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstage.jst.go.jp/article/jpi/60/2/60_85/_article/-char/ja/" rel="nofollow">"Evaluation of Clay and Fumed Silica Nanoparticles on Adsorption of Surfactant Polymer during Enhanced Oil Recovery"</a>. <i>Journal of the Japan Petroleum Institute</i> (in Japanese). <b>60</b> (2): 85–94. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1627%2Fjpi.60.85" rel="nofollow">10.1627/jpi.60.85</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1346-8804" rel="nofollow">1346-8804</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+the+Japan+Petroleum+Institute&amp;rft.atitle=Evaluation+of+Clay+and+Fumed+Silica+Nanoparticles+on+Adsorption+of+Surfactant+Polymer+during+Enhanced+Oil+Recovery&amp;rft.volume=60&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=85-94&amp;rft.date=2017&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1627%2Fjpi.60.85&amp;rft.issn=1346-8804&amp;rft.aulast=Cheraghian&amp;rft.aufirst=Goshtasp&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstage.jst.go.jp%2Farticle%2Fjpi%2F60%2F2%2F60_85%2F_article%2F-char%2Fja%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APetroleum"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a class="external text" href="http://www.eia.gov/analysis/petroleum/crudetypes/pdf/crudetypes.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>U.S. Crude Oil Production Forecast- Analysis of Crude Types</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>, Washington, DC: U.S. Energy Information Administration, May 28, 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 13,</span> 2018</span>, <q>U.S. oil production has grown rapidly in recent years. U.S. Energy Information Administration (EIA) data, which reflect combined production of crude oil and lease condensate, show a rise from 5.6 million barrels per day (bbl/d) in 2011 to 7.5 million bbl/d in 2013, and a record 1.2 million bbl/d increase to 8.7 million bbl/d in 2014. Increasing production of light crude oil in low-permeability or tight resource formations in regions like the Bakken, Permian Basin, and Eagle Ford (often referred to as light tight oil) account for nearly all the net growth in U.S. crude oil production.<br/>EIA’s latest Short-Term Energy Outlook, issued in May 2015, reflects continued production growth in 2015 and 2016, albeit at a slower pace than in 2013 and 2014, with U.S. crude oil production in 2016 forecast to reach 9.2 million bbl/d. Beyond 2016, the Annual Energy Outlook 2015 (AEO2015) projects further production growth, although its pace and duration remains highly uncertain.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=U.S.+Crude+Oil+Production+Forecast-+Analysis+of+Crude+Types&amp;rft.place=Washington%2C+DC&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Energy+Information+Administration&amp;rft.date=2015-05-28&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.eia.gov%2Fanalysis%2Fpetroleum%2Fcrudetypes%2Fpdf%2Fcrudetypes.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APetroleum"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Ovale, Peder. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141213105654/http://www.tu.no/petroleum/2014/12/11/her-ser-du-hvorfor-oljeprisen-faller" rel="nofollow">"Her ser du hvorfor oljeprisen faller"</a>.
